The structure of the thesis is of a smaller main article containing the central parts of the

whole. Due to the structure of the thesis, the paper contains two abstracts. One concise
version as part of the article, and an extended version for the thesis in itŌĆÖs entirety.

Abstract
The dynamic properties of a beam structures is an essential part of structural design, as the
e’¼Ćect of resonant loading can prove catastrophic. The use of thin-walled beams is prevalent
within the ’¼üelds of structural, mechanical and aeronautical engineering. Circumstances in
each ’¼üeld may dictate that the use of beams with complex geometry is advantageous. The
use of beams of asymmetric cross-sections is inherently associated with the coupling of vibra-
tions, due to the none-coinciding shear- and elastic centres. The present paper consider the
triply coupled bending-torsion vibration problem encountered when utilizing fully asymmetric
sections. The vibration problem is presented as an eigenvalue problem by deriving e’¼Ćective
mass and sti’¼Ćness matrices to describe the system.
The present theory accounts for the
warping e’¼Ćect due to inhomogeneous torsion, while neglecting shear deformations and rotary
inertia. It is assumed that the coupled vibration modes may be accurately approximated as
linear combinations of the ordinary un-coupled vibration modes. As asymmetric section are
associated with principle-axes which does not align with geometry, the present theory utilize
none-principle axes for the description of the beam. This allows for both loading and the
boundary conditions to be described in terms of the geometric axes, which is generally how
beams are installed in practice. The matrix formulation of the problem allows the study of
forced vibration response by Newmark time-integration.
The present theory is implemented in a simple Matlab program named ViCoBeam. The
accuracy of the theory and related solution are veri’¼üed both numerically and experimen-
tally.
An ABAQUS ’¼ünite element model of a thin-walled asymmetric cantilever beam is
produced, and the resulting eigenfrequencies are compared with corresponding frequencies of
ViCoBeam. It is found that the natural frequencies of both are in agreement. Additional
veri’¼ücation is performed by alternate boundary condition set-ups. It is found that utilizing
varying boundary condition for each of the spacial function of the two-directional bending
vibration problem is associated with inaccuracies.
The results are veri’¼üed experimentally, by the testing of an asymmetric aluminium cantilever
beam, corresponding the the previous FEM model. Hammer stroke testing shows that the
natural frequencies of the beam are lower than those found by ViCoBeam. A white-noise test
by an attached vibration exciter con’¼ürms these ’¼ünding. The theoretical boundary condition
are adjusted accordingly by implementing elastic boundary conditions and the correct fre-
quencies are reproduced. However the same problem of utilizing alternate bending boundary
conditions is encountered. The vibration modes are studied by measuring the response accel-
erations given excitation at speci’¼üc frequencies. It is found that Newmark time-integration
using elastic boundary conditions produce matching response accelerations at the ’¼ürst vibra-
tion mode. However, the higher vibration modes cannot be reproduced.
The future development of the present theory is brie’¼éy discussed and the extension needed
to include rotary inertia e’¼Ćects and the more accurate coupled bending vibration modes is
outlined.

Resum┬┤e
De dynamiske egenskaber af en bj├”lke konstruktion er en essentiel del af strukturel de-
sign, da e’¼Ćekterne af resonans belastning kan v├”re katastrofale.
Brugen af tyndv├”ggede
bj├”lker er almindeligt idenfor de strukturelle, mekaniske of aeronautiske ingeni├Ėromr╦Üader.
Omst├”ndigheder idenfor de enkelte felter kan betyde at brugen af bj├”lker med kompleks ge-
ometri er fordelagtigt. Brugen af bj├”lker med asymmetriske tv├”rsnit er naturligt forbundet
med koblede vibrationer p╦Üagrund af de ikke-sammenfaldne elastiske- og forskydningscentre.
Det n├”rv├”rende projekt omhandler det tredobbelt koblede b├Ėjnings-torsion svingningsprob-
lem, der opst╦Üar n╦Üar fuld asymmetriske bj├”lker benyttes. Svingningsproblemet er opstillet
som en egenv├”rdis problem ved at udlede de tilsvarende masse og stivheds matricer der
beskriver systemet.
Den fremstillede teori tager h├Ėjde for hv├”lvningse’¼Ćekterne som f├Ėlge
af inhomogen vridning, men negligere forskydningsdeformationer og rotations inerti.
Det
antages at de koblede svingningsformer kan beskrives som en line├”r kombination af de or-
din├”re afkoblede svingningsformer. Da asymmetriske tv├”rsnit er associerede med hovedakser
der ikke er parallel med tv├”rsnitsgeometrien, bruges ikke-hovedakser til at beskrive bj├”lken.
Dette muligg├Ėre at b╦Üade laster og randbetingelser der er beskrevet ud fra de geometriske akser,
hvilket generelt er hvordan bj├”lker er forbundet i praksis. Matrix formuleringen af svingn-

Den forel├”ggende teori er implementeret i et simpelt MATLAB program kaldt ViCoBeam.
Pr├”cisionen af teorien of dertilh├Ėrende resultater er eftervist b╦Üade numerisk og eksperimen-
talt. En ABAQUS ’¼ünite-element model af en tyndv├”gget asymmetrisk udkraget bj├”lke er
opstillet og de resulterende egenfrekvenser er sammenlignet med tilsvarende frekvenser fra
ViCoBeam. Det konstanternes at resultaterne passer overens. Yderliggere eftervisning er

hver er to retningsbestemte b├Ėjningsfunktioner er forbundet med afvigelser.
Resultaterne eftervises eksperimentalt ved at teste en asymmetrisk udkraget aluminiumsb-
j├”lke det passer med den foreg╦Üaende FEM model. Test med hammerslag viser at bj├”lkens
egenfrekvenser er lavere end hvad ViCoBeam producerer. En hvid-st├Ėj test med en tilkoblet
vibrations shaker bekr├”fter disse fund. De teoretiske randbetingelser justeres ved at imple-
mentere elastiske randbetingelser og de korrekte egenfrekvenser gen’¼ündes. Igen st├Ėdes der dog
p╦Üaproblemet med brugen af varierende b├Ėjningsrandbetingelser. Bj├”lkens Svingningsformer
er unders├Ėgt ved at m╦Üale svingningsaccelerationerne ved rystelser med speci’¼ük frekvens. Det
ses at Newmark tids-integrationen af bj├”lken med elastiske randbetingelser passer overens
med den f├Ėrste svingningsform. Det h├Ėjere svingningsformer kan dog ikke reproduceres.
Videreudviklingen af teorien er kort diskuteret og et udkast til den n├Ėdvendige udvidelse for
at medtage rotations inerti of koblede b├Ėjningssvingninger er pr├”senteret.

Coupled bending-torsion vibrations of thin-walled beams with
asymmetric cross sections
Christian Cr┬©uger Ahm
Department of Mechanical Engineering
Technical University of Denmark
July 2015
Abstract
A solution of the coupled bending-torsion vibration problem is presented as an eigen-
value problem based on modi’¼üed Bernoulli-Euler beam theory, including torsional warping
e’¼Ćects, but neglecting rotary inertia and shear deformations. The solution assumes that
the coupled vibration modes may be represented as serials expansions of the uncoupled
modes. The proposed solution is implemented with the Matlab program, ViCoBeam.
The results are veri’¼üed numerically by a FEM model and found to be in agreement. The
matrix formulation of the problem allows for time-history analysis of forced vibrations by
Newmark integration. The results are veri’¼üed experimentally by measuring the natural
frequencies and forced vibration response of a cantilever beam. The resulting natural fre-
quencies are found to be in agreement when describing the beam using elastic boundary
conditions, but poor correlation of the observed response accelerations and the calculated
time-history is found. The solution method is found to show signi’¼ücant deviations when
utilizing di’¼Ćerent boundary conditions for the two bending modes.
1
Introduction
The problem of coupled vibrations is present in all construction where the use of beams of
asymmetric cross-sections present. Thin-walled open cross section are extensively used as
structural components within the ’¼üelds of civil, mechanical and aeronautical engineering, and
asymmetric cross sections are prevalent within all three ’¼üelds. Asymmetric beams commonly
have none-coinciding shear- and elastic centres which inherently causes coupling between
bending and torsional vibrations. The degree of bending-torsion coupling is determined by
number of symmetry lines. A mono-symmetric cross-section induce coupling between the
torsional vibrations and one of two transverse bending vibrations. This is typically refereed
to as double coupling, while triple coupling refers to the complete coupling of the torsional
problem and both bending vibration problems, associated with complete asymmetry.
The coupled vibration problem has been documented throughout the past century and solu-
tions are still studied. Early studies of aeroplane wings by Myklestad [1] and Mendelson and
Gendler [2] proposed solutions by discretizing the beam into a ’¼ünite number of stations. Gere
and Lin [3] formulate an early solution method using the Rayleigh-Ritz method to derive the
natural frequencies. The solution of Gere and Lin [3] is based on a modi’¼üed Bernoulli-Euler
beam theory accounting for warping e’¼Ćect, but neglecting the e’¼Ćects and shear deformation
and rotational inertia. Several studies of the coupled vibration problem makes a priori elimi-
nation of coupling e’¼Ćects by either studying speci’¼üc cases, such as the study of open channels
by Aggarwal and Cranch [4] and Yaman [5][6], or the study of equal angle sections by Hasan
and Barr[7][8] - or exclude various e’¼Ćects such as rotary inertia and shear deformations such
as Gere and Lin [3]. Vlasov [9] formulated theory for coupled vibration which include the
inertial e’¼Ćects of bending rotation but neglect the shear deformations. Muller [10] derived a
1
==PAGE:16==
general theory that in principle account for all kinematic coupling e’¼Ćects but is excessively
complex and has not commonly been adopted in later studies. An exact solution was pre-
sented by Dokumaci [11] but the solution neglects several e’¼Ćects, including warping. The
solution is later expanded by Bishop et al [12] to include warping e’¼Ćect, but only for doubly
coupled vibrations. Further development of the solution was done by Tanaka and Bercin [13],
extending the solution to triple coupled thin-walled beams for several boundary condition
using Mathematica. However, this extension to the triple coupled problems does not account
for the change in principle axes, associated with nonsymmetric cross-section. This is later
corrected by Arpaci and Bozdag [14] as the coupling sti’¼Ćness EIzy is introduced. Arpaci et
al [15] further expand the solution to include rotary inertia. The same study investigates
the e’¼Ćect of neglecting or including rotary inertia and warping e’¼Ćects, and ’¼ünd the e’¼Ćects
signi’¼ücant. The inclusion of shear deformations have similarly been studied in several papers,
Figure 1: Coupled bending-torsion vibration mode
typically by utilizing Timoshenko beam theory. Bishop and Price [16] studied the coupled
vibrations of Timoshenko-beams, but neglected warping e’¼Ćects. Ambrosini et al [17][18] de-
veloped a modi’¼üed Vlasov theory, utilizing the Timoshenko shear deformations and solve the
vibration problem using a state variables approach. Li et al [19] have presented a dynamic
transfer matrix method to determined frequencies and mode shapes of axially loaded Timo-
shenko beams including both shear deformations and rotary inertia. However, the study is
limited to mono-symmetric beams. Prokic [20] presents what appears to be the only analysis
of fully coupled non-symmetric Timoshenko beams, including the ’¼üvefold coupling of two
directional bending-shearing and torsion.
Chen and Hsiao [21] analyse the addition axial
coupling that occur when the boudary condition are not de’¼üned by the elastic center. The
cross-sectional distortion associated with shear deformations are commonly neglected in vi-
bration problems. The inherent complexity of cross-sectional distortion makes formulation
of a universal distortion function di’¼ācult. As such, study of shear distortion in vibration
problems has been limited to speci’¼üc beam sections such as equal angle-sections by Hasan
and Barr[7] and H-sections by Barr and Duthie [22]. A common simpli’¼ücation of the various
solutions of the coupled vibration problem, is the use of principle axes. This is well known
from statics to decouple the bending functions. However, for triple coupled vibration prob-
lems of asymmetric beam cross-sections, the principle axes does not typically align with the
beam geometry, and it may be advantageous to describe the beam in terms of an alternate
coordinate system. Lin [23] makes one of the ’¼ürst use of none-principle axes for the coupled
vibration problem, when expanding the his work with Gere [3] to the speci’¼üc case of beams
with restricted motion in one plane.
The present paper propose a solution method by presenting the coupled vibration problem
2
==PAGE:17==
as an eigenvalue problem, drawing inspiration from the Rayleigh-Ritz method utilized by
Gere and Lin [3]. The proposed solution utilize none-principle axes as originally presented
by Lin [23]. This allows for the boundary conditions to be de’¼üned in axes corresponding to
practical installations of the beam rather than the principle axes. The matrix formulation
of the problem allows for a time-history analysis of forced vibrations, similar to the BEM
method utilized by Sapountzakis and Tsiatas [24].
The theory is based on the modi’¼üed
Bernoulli-Euler beam theory for uniform beams including warping e’¼Ćect, while neglecting
e’¼Ćect such as rotary inertia and shear deformation, similar to Gere and Lin [3]. The results of
the proposed theory are veri’¼üed numerically by comparison with a FEM model. In addition,
experimental veri’¼ücation is done by testing the dynamic properties of a asymmetric cantilever
beam. Experimental veri’¼ücation is generally scarce in literature. A noticeable exception to
this was carried out by Ambrosini [25].
2
Mechanical Energy Consideration
To present the coupled vibration problem as an eigenvalue problem, the associated system
matrices must be derived. This requires speci’¼üc evaluation of the mechanical energy of the
system.
As such, the kinetic and elastic energy associated with beam vibration must be
expressed.
2.1
Beam Kinematics
To evaluate the mechanical energy of a beam, it is essential to accurately describe the kinemat-
ics of the beam, so that the displacement ’¼üeld may be represented by generalized translation,
rotation and warping.
As described by Muller [10], the de’¼ünition of the kinematic relations of the beam, determines
which e’¼Ćects are included in the eventual solution to the vibration problem. If the kinematic
relations are de’¼üned such that a speci’¼üc e’¼Ćect is neglected, one cannot recover it without
losing any possible coupling e’¼Ćects that might occur. The following derivation follows the
y, v
x, w
z, u
Figure 2: Beam with coordinates and displacement components
work of Krenk [26]. As illustrated in Figure 2, A straight beam of constant cross section
is considered. The x-axis is placed parallel to the beam orientation, while the z, y-axes are
placed in the plane of the beam cross-section. The displacement ’¼üeld of the beam is described
in terms of the components u, v and w - representing the transverse- and axial displacement
respectively.

2.3

energy has two components, associated with the axial strain ╬Ą and the St. Venant shear strain
╬│ respectively.

(2.11)

from the St. Venant shear strain

3
The Eigenvalue Problem

mechanical energy consideration. This follows the Rayleigh-Ritz method [27]. It is assumed

ration of variables and serial expansion using the known uncoupled mode shapes Zn(x), Yn(x)
and ╬”n(x) as spacial solutions and unknown temporal solution rzn(t), ryn(t) and sn(t).

3.1
N-convergence
Theoretically, the displacement and rotation functions ╬Šz(x, t),╬Šy(x, t) and Žå(x, t) require the
in’¼ünite serial expansion of (3.1) to produce correct results. As this is not feasible for calcula-
tion a limited number of terms N is used. By noting the structure of the system matrices M
and K, it is clear that the degree of serial expansion de’¼üned by N determines the coupling
between lower and higher vibration modes through the coupling matrices kzy and ╬©, as the
11
==PAGE:26==
remaining matrices are diagonal. This is re’¼éected in the resulting natural frequencies deter-
mined by the eigenvalue problem (3), which will vary on the degree of expansion N. The
frequencies will converge towards the correct values as N increases and the coupling e’¼Ćect of
additional higher vibration modes are included. The rate of convergence will vary on the sim-
ilarity the two bending- and the rotation shape functions. In the present context, similarity
refers to the ability of two functions to ful’¼ül the orthogonality conditions of either function.
Therefore it will depend on both boundary conditions and sti’¼Ćness parameters. If the two
spacial bending functions Y (x) and Z(x) are de’¼üned by the same set of boundary conditions,
they ful’¼ül the orthogonality criterion and the coupling sti’¼Ćness matrix kzy becomes diago-
nal. This reduces the coupling between lower and high order vibration modes to the inertial
coupling matrices ╬©. This inter-modal coupling can be further reduced if the rotation and
bending shape functions are similar, as this makes the inner product between di’¼Ćerent order
of modes approach zero.
Ō¤©Z(x)j, ╬”(x)nŌ¤®=
Z
Z(x)j╬”(x)ndx ŌåÆ0
,
j ╠Ė= n
(3.28)
As the rotational and bending vibration problem are inherently di’¼Ćerent full similarity and
diagonal inertial coupling matrices ╬© cannot be achieved. However, as the sti’¼Ćness ratio
k2 = GK
EIŽē ŌåÆ0 the governing spacial di’¼Ćerential equations (See Appendix A) and their so-
lution become increasingly similar, thus reducing the di’¼Ćerence in shape function to the
boundary conditions. In practise the boundary conditions of bending and rotation problems
are often similar - such as for a cantilever, where both have one end de’¼üned by static bound-
ary conditions and one de’¼üned by kinematic boundary conditions. Figure 5 illustrates the
di’¼Ćerence in the rate of convergence between 3 di’¼Ćerent beams. Figure 5a shows 3 di’¼Ćerent
boundary conditions. Beam 1 has simply-supported torsional boundary conditions, restricting
rotation but not warping. The transverse boundary conditions correspond to ’¼üxed-’¼üxed in
one bending direction and simple-simple in the other. Beam 2 is simply-supported in regards
to bending in both directions, while ’¼üxed-’¼üxed in regards to torsion. Beam 3 is an ordinary
cantilever.

4.3
Forced vibration response
An advantage of the matrix formulation of the vibration problem, is the application of New-
mark integration to calculate the time-history of beam vibration given a harmonic load.
However doing so requires the energy balance (3.25) to be extended to include the rate of
work from the external loads Df, in addition to any energy dissipation through damping Ds.
╦ÖV + ╦ÖT = Ds + Df
(4.16)

4.3.3
Localized mass
For realistic implementation of an external load, additional mass will often be added to the
system, which will increase the inertia of the system. An example of this would be the the
mass of the moving element of a vibration exciter and attachments needed to secure it to the
beam. The additional inertial contribution from these parts must added to mass matrix of
the system M. The kinetic energy Tf associated with this added mass can be described by
the following expression, where axial displacement has been omitted. This neglects the rotary
inertia of the additional mass, similarly to the kinetic energy of the beam itself in Section 2.2.
Tf = 1
2mf( ╦Öu(xf)2 + ╦Öv(xf)2)
(4.26)

the kinematic expression of the displacements (2.1) and (2.2) gives following expression of the
additional kinetic energy, while introducing (emz, emy) as the location of the center of mass
and the additional mass, in relation to the shear center.

4.3.4
Damping
To achieve realistic response functions energy dissipation through damping must be introduced
to the system. This is to inhibit that the external loading from increasing the mechanical
energy inde’¼ünitely. Therefore a simple damping mechanism in the form of Rayleigh damping
is implemented.
Rayleigh damping is a sti’¼Ćness and mass proportional damping mechanic that is intended to
represents the internal damping from the straining of the material in addition to damping by
air resistance.
For a simple single degree-of-freedom, the energy dissipation though viscous damping is ex-
pressed as
Ds = ŌłÆcv2
(4.32)
Where c is the damping coe’¼ācient. This cannot directly by applied to a continuous system
such as a beam. Instead the energy dissipation can be described as the volume integral of a
damping density C, containing both the damping parameter and the squared velocity.
Ds =
Z
V
CdV
(4.33)
By introducing the Rayleigh damping parameters ╬▒ and ╬▓, the damping density is expressed
as the sum of two terms, representing the mass and sti’¼Ćness proportionality. The values of
the ╬▒ and ╬▓ determine the damping ratio ╬Č as described in Appendix C. It is noted that this
is a distinctly di’¼Ćerent de’¼ünition of ╬Č than used in Section 2.1.
C = ╬▒Žü ╦Öu2 + ╬▓(E ╦Ö╬Ą2 + G╦Ö╬│2)
(4.34)

(4.35)
By applying the same derivation as performed for the kinetic and elastic energy in Section
2.2 and 2.3 respectively, the energy dissipation may be expressed as a matrix product
Ds = ŌłÆ╦ÖqT C ╦Öq
(4.36)
Where the damping matrix C follows the classic Rayleigh damping structure

Inserting the above expression (4.37) in addition to the expression of the rate of external work

╦ÖqT M┬©q + ╦ÖqT C ╦Öq + ╦ÖqT Kq = ╦ÖqT f(t)
(4.38)
As previously to achieve none-trivial solutions this must apply for all instances of ╦Öq(t) thus
the following system of equations must be ful’¼ülled.
M┬©q + C ╦Öq + Kq = f(t)
(4.39)
4.3.5
Time-history
By implementing damping and external loading, the time-history of the beam vibration is
determined using Newmark time-integration. The exact procedure of the time-integration
is expanded in Appendix G. The time history allows for for speci’¼üc solutions of the state
vector q(t) at the speci’¼üc time t. Due to the nature of Newmark integration, corresponding
acceleration and and velocity vectors ┬©q(t), ╦Öq(t) are determined simultaneously. Through linear
combination of the shape functions Zn(x), Yn(x) and ╬”n(x) this provides exact values of the
three DOFs ╬Šz, ╬Šy and Žå at each time increment along the length of the beam.
Sample time-histories of the end point acceleration of a cantilever beam is found in Figure
9. The ’¼ügure shows the response at two excitation frequencies; the ’¼ürst resonant frequency
Žēf = Žē1 and at a o’¼Ć-set frequency of Žēf = 0.9 Žē1

5
Numerical veri’¼ücation
The present theory is implemented within the Matlab program named ViCoBeam. The
program consists of a simple assembly of the system matrices in accordance with the present
20
==PAGE:35==
theory, based on pre-de’¼üned shape functions and section parameters, in addition to the so-
lution of the eigenvalue problem and assembly of response functions as seen in Appendix
K. To verify the accuracy of the theory and implementation, a ’¼ünite-element model of a
thin-walled beam is produced along with the later experimental veri’¼ücation. This is done
using the commercial ’¼ünite-element program - ABAQUS. The test-beam is modelled using
shell elements and a eigenfrequency analysis is run. To verify the FEM model accuracy, a
simple I-beam with well de’¼üned analytical natural frequencies is modelled and the frequencies
compared. This veri’¼ücation along with the detailed description of the modelling can be found
in Appendix D.

6
Experimental veri’¼ücation
To further verify the accuracy of ViCoBeam and the present theory, an experiment is set up
to verify the ’¼ündings. As previously mentioned, practical veri’¼ücations of coupled vibration
problems are scarce is literature. As solutions should be applicable in practical structural
use, it is essential to show correlation between theoretical solutions and experimental obser-
vations. An asymmetric aluminium beam is designed, corresponding to full triple coupling
between bending and torsion vibrations. The beam cross section can be seen in Figure 10
23
==PAGE:38==
To ’¼ünd accurate experimental results corresponding to those of ViCoBeam, the actual sup-
porting connections of the beams should match the theoretical boundary conditions. The
theoretical boundary conditions assumes perfect rigidity of the kinematic degrees-of-freedom
at the boundaries. In practice, no boundary conditions will be perfectly rigid but rather have
elastic behaviour to some extend. As such, careful considerations must be made when design-
ing the supports. The simplest ideal boundary condition to implement in practice, is a free
end as no rigidity is assumed. As such a cantilever con’¼üguration is chosen, as this requires
only one end of beam to be considered. As explained in Section 5 and expanded further in
Section F, the present theory and therefore ViCoBeam, will not produce accurate results
for boundary condition con’¼ügurations that imply di’¼Ćerent shape function of the two ’¼éexural
modes. An ordinary cantilever con’¼üguration implies that both transverse degrees-of-freedom
have ’¼üxed-free boundary conditions, thus satisfying this requirement. Detailed description
of the experimental set-up can be found in Appendix E. The experimental veri’¼ücation is
Figure 13: Illustration of experiment set-up
done in three parts. One hammer stroke test to determine the natural frequencies of the free
vibrations. One white-noise test to determine the same frequencies when the beam is con-
nected to the vibration exciter. Finally an excitation test of speci’¼üc resonant frequencies is
performed to study the mode shapes of the vibrations by comparing the ratios of the vertical
and horizontal accelerations.
6.1
Hammer stroke test
To excite the free vibrations of the beam, a series of hammer strokes are performed and
the vibrations recorded through the two MEMS accelerometers from PCB. The impact of the
hammer blow induces mechanical energy to the system forcing a dynamic response. The beam
will naturally excite a broad range of frequencies contain excitation of each of the natural
frequencies as shown by analysis of the dynamic response of impulse loadings (Inman [30]).
The beam is struck by hammer blows with 2 di’¼Ćerent hammers across multiple locations on
beam as described in Appendix E. The natural frequencies are found by evaluating the power
density spectrum found by Fast Fourier Transform of the measured response accelerations.
Figure 14 shows the logarithmic power density spectrum for two tests that are representative
of the vertical and horizontal response of the of the beam. The mean values of the frequencies
found by each of the tests can be found in Table 4 along with the corresponding resulting
frequencies of ViCoBeam. The resulting frequencies of ViCoBeam are seen for three cases:
The beam on itŌĆÖs own, the beam including the mass of the accelerometers, and the beam with
24
==PAGE:39==
accelerometers assuming unrestricted warping at the ’¼üxed end.

the modes shapes found in Table 12a of Section D.1. Furthermore, It can it seen that the

in the PSD of speci’¼üc hammer tests, however none that appear consistently and are likely
due to aliasing of higher order modes.

can be seen that the test frequencies are signi’¼ücantly lower than those of ViCoBeam. This
does not match the ’¼ündings of previous comparison to the ABAQUS model in Table 3. As
it is assumed that the FEM model can be considered accurate, this suggests that there are
some e’¼Ćects that are both present in ViCoBeam and the FEM model that are not present
in the actual beam set-up.

Including the inertia related to the mass of the accelerometers improves the results, but not
su’¼āciently. Another plausible cause of the discrepancy is overestimation of the rigidity of the
boundary conditions. Both ViCoBeam and the FEM model assumes perfect rigidity of the
supports. In reality supports are never perfectly rigid and especially warping is di’¼ācult to fully
restrict due to the small axial deformations (See Appendix H). As such the torsional boundary
25
==PAGE:40==
conditions of ViCoBeam are adjusted so that warping in unrestricted thus achieving zero
bi-moment at the ’¼üxed end. It terms of boundary conditions this implies that ŽåŌĆ▓ŌĆ▓(0) = 0.
It is clear that this shows signi’¼ücant improvement of the results, especially for the torsional
dominated modes. In fact it is seen that the third ViCoBeam mode at f3 = 83.4 is now
marginally lower than the corresponding test results. While fully freeing warping of the ’¼üxed
end shows signi’¼ücant improvements to the results, it does not fully represent the physical
system. As such it is reasonable that a torsionally dominated mode will be underestimated
with completely unrestricted warping.
6.2
White-noise test
To further study the dynamic response of the beam, an vibration exciter is connected to
study the dynamic behaviour at speci’¼üc excitation frequencies.
The exciter is connected
at xf = l/3. By connecting the vibration exciter, the e’¼Ćective dynamic properties of the
beam inherently change.
This is due to the additional mass contribution of the moving
element of the exciter along with the transfer joint. ViCoBeam allows for implementation
of localized mass, and is adjusted to account for these additional inertial e’¼Ćects.
As the
dynamic properties are changed, the natural frequencies of the hammer stroke test will not
be accurate. To determine the altered natural frequencies of the combined system, a white-
noise test is performed. The vibration exciter is set to generate white-noise excitation. As
white-noise have a constant power density spectrum, any peaks in the measured PSD will
be due to the dynamic behaviour of the beam which is biased toward itŌĆÖs natural vibration
modes. Details of the white-noise test can be found in Appendix E. The resulting vertical
and horizontal PSD can be seen in Figure 15.
The test is performed with two di’¼Ćerent
location of the accelerometers; at end- and at mid-point. The resulting mean frequencies can
be seen in Table 5. It can be seen that generally the frequencies decrease. This correspond

To account for this, the boundary conditions are adjusted to assume an elastic reaction
bending moment with the sti’¼Ćness constant KM (See section A) and an elastic reaction bi-
moment with the sti’¼Ćness constant KB. This allows the solution to be adjusted, so that
the agreement of the second mode is improved. The results of this adjustment is similarly
seen in Table 6.
It is clear, that attempting to adjust the boundary sti’¼Ćness to improve
agreement of the 2nd vibration, has signi’¼ücantly decreased the accuracy of the ’¼ürst natural
frequency. This suggests that the boundary bending sti’¼Ćness cannot be accurately described
in both directions with the same sti’¼Ćness constant KM. To account for this, varying sti’¼Ćness
parameters KMz and KMy are applied to each boundary condition of the two directions. This
allows the results to be adjust more precisely as seen in Table 7.
The need to adjust the sti’¼Ćness of the boundary conditions emphasize the importance of
accurately describing the physical boundary conditions when performing a dynamic analysis.
The initial intention of the welded connection, was to sett up an experiment with more de’¼üned
warping boundary conditions in comparison to other studies. This has not been successful,

6.3
Excitation of resonant frequencies
While the natural frequencies can be accurately determined by either hammer stroke or white-
noise testing, the mode shapes require more careful study of the resonant response at speci’¼üc
frequencies. This is done by studying the magnitude of the acceleration in each direction
at a resonant frequency. To determine the exact mode shapes of the beam experimentally,
several accelerometers along the beam are needed. As the system consist of 3 degrees of
freedom - rotation Žå, vertical translation ╬Šy and horizontal translation ╬Šz - 3 accelerometers
at several points along the beam, are ideally needed to determine the mode shapes.
As
a limited number of accelerometers are available this is not feasible. Instead, the ratio of
vertical and horizontal acceleration of the experiment - denoted as Žć - is compared to those
found from the Newmark time-integration. Both accelerations will consist of contributions
from the rotation in addition to the related translation as described from the kinematic
relations (2.1). As several combinations of Žå, ╬Šy and ╬Šz will result in the same vertical and
horizontal accelerations, the mode shapes cannot be de’¼ünitively veri’¼üed. Instead the study
will purely add con’¼üdence to the solutions of ViCoBeam rather than con’¼ürm them entirely.
The comparison of accelerations is done for the two di’¼Ćerent locations on the beam xaccel =
0.98m and xaccel = 0.50m. The change of location of the accelerometers will inherently lead
to some change in natural frequency as seen in Table 6. As such the resonant response of the
beam will occur at a slightly di’¼Ćerent frequency for each of the two locations. This will have
an impact for the magnitude of the measured accelerations. Therefore, the measurement at
the two location cannot be directly compared to each other without some inherent inaccuracy.
For consistency, the testing of mid point accelerations is done at the same excitation frequency
as used for the end point accelerations.
Initially the beam is excited at the ’¼ürst natural frequency as determined by the white-noise
test and the response is studied.
The resulting vertical and horizontal accelerations are
seen in Figure 16.

boundary conditions.

From Table 8 it clear that large deviations exist beween the observed and the calculated

The Newmark time-integration with the elastic boundary conditions
produce accelerations with reasonable accuracy for the 1st mode. However large di’¼Ćerences
are seen for the higher modes. While these elastic boundary conditions generally produce the
smallest discrepancy with test results, they do not match with any satisfactory accuracy. It
is noted that the observed accelerations suggest that the vertical and horizontal accelerations
are of opposite direction for both the 3rd and 4th mode. By comparison, this is only seen
for Newmark integration at the end-point of the 4th mode. It is found that the Newmark
integration produces time-histories with vertical and horizontal accelerations out of phase
for several boundary conditions. This is not observed in any of test results. Generally it
seem, that while the ’¼ürst vibration mode matches well with the mode of the elastic boundary
conditions, the higher modes does not seem to match any of the calculated time-histories.
More detailed study of the speci’¼üc accelerations of the 3 DOFs along the beam is needed to
identify the precise disagreement.
31

7
Discussion
The comparison with the numerical results of the ABAQUS model of Table 3 show agreement
with the natural frequencies of ViCoBeam for the cantilever beam. The discrepancy across
the ’¼ürst 6 modes corresponds with expectations due to the simpli’¼ücations of Bernoulli-Euler
beam theory, as all theoretical frequencies are found to be slightly higher than the numerical
results. This is represented in both frequencies and mode shapes. The alternative simple/’¼üxed
boundary condition however, show to have signi’¼ücant deviation at the ’¼ürst mode of 9.2%.
The ’¼ürst frequency of ViCoBeam are found to be lower than the corresponding numerical
frequency. This cannot be explained by the shortcomings of the beam theory. Both the neglect
of shear deformations and rotary inertia should result in an increase of the natural frequencies,
as is seen for cantilever beam. The inaccuracy of this set of boundary conditions are similarly
evident in the corresponding mode shapes of Figure 12b. The inaccuracy identi’¼üed for this
set of boundary conditions can be attributed to the use of uncoupled mode shapes. The
assumption that justi’¼ües the use of uncoupled mode shapes is that the boundary conditions
of the coupled and uncoupled modes are identical. However, by utilizing none-principle axis
and introducing sti’¼Ćness coupling between the two bending functions, the static boundary
conditions change. This is detailed in Appendix F. This boundary problem is avoided by
utilizing identical boundary conditions for both bending problems, as is the case for the
cantilever beam. This allows the shape functions to ful’¼ül orthogonality criterion thus making
the coupling sti’¼Ćness matrix kzy diagonal.
Evaluating the resulting mode shapes of the
simple/’¼üxed boundary conditions, also show larger disparity between the bending functions
than what was evident for the cantilever beam. This supports the explanation of altered
boundary conditions for coupled bending problem, as the un-coupled mode shapes no longer
describe the coupled vibration problem with su’¼ācient accuracy.
While the ABAQUS model generally con’¼ürms the results of ViCoBeam, the experimental
veri’¼ücation does not show similar agreement. The experimental frequencies of the hammer
stroke test are lower than the corresponding calculated frequencies. This suggests that the
mathematical model either underestimates the e’¼Ćective inertia of the system or overestimates
the sti’¼Ćness of the system. As the ABAQUS model of the cantilever con’¼ürm the ViCoBeam
results, the disparity must be present in both models. By altering the theoretical boundary
conditions and comparing the resulting frequencies, it seems that the welded connection is
not su’¼āciently rigid for the beam to be described as a perfect cantilever. By implementing
an elastic reaction bi-moment at the ’¼üxed end, the exact sti’¼Ćness of boundary condition can
be adjusted to better match the physical system. Figure 18 shows the resulting frequencies
with varying boundary warping sti’¼Ćness parameter KB (See section A). KB = 0 corresponds
to fully unrestricted warping, while KB ŌåÆŌł×correspond to the fully rigid cantilever case.
The results of Table 4 suggest that the welded support conditions does very little to restrict
the torsional warping. The torsional dominated modes are naturally more e’¼Ćected by altering
the KB parameter. However, due to the coupled nature of the vibration problem, all modes
will be e’¼Ćected to some extend. As such, Determining any precise value of the KB parameter,
will depend on the bending boundary conditions. As the bending dominated modes still show
poor accuracy, improvement to these modes is necessary.
As it seems that the welding does little to restrict warping, it can be suspected that the
welding does not fully restrict bending rotation similarly, leading to the implementation of
elastic reaction moments.
Table 7 shows that implementing di’¼Ćerent sti’¼Ćness parameters
in each direction results in frequencies in agreement with the results of the white-noise test.
When evaluating the welded connection (Appendix I) it seems sensible that bending resistance
is greater for the vertical bending than for horizontal. If it assumed that the reaction moment

is carried exclusively by the ’¼éanges, it is clear that larger portion of the weld is used to
restrict the vertical rotation than horizontal. Analogously the vertical moment of inertia is
similarly larger then the horizontal. However, as discussed previously and detailed further in
Appendix F, the use of di’¼Ćerent boundary conditions in the two-directional bending problem
does not ful’¼ül the boundary conditions and results in inaccuracies. As such, the use of di’¼Ćerent
boundary sti’¼Ćness parameters KMz ╠Ė= KMy, while seemingly accurate, cannot necessarily be
justi’¼üed. This highlights the need for developing coupled mode shapes for the present theory
to allow for accurate description of the boundary conditions.
It is noted that the attachment of the vibration exciter leads to an increase in the observed
frequencies of the two ’¼ürst vibration modes. This is contrary of what is expected, as the
increased inertia of the moving element and connecting join should lead to decreased fre-
quencies. One hypothesis is that the membrane of the vibration exciter provides additional
sti’¼Ćness, which would mainly impact the vertical translation dominated mode such as the
’¼ürst mode, which has the largest increase. However as the increase is as much as 9% it seems
unlikely that membrane sti’¼Ćness would account for such a signi’¼ücant increase. It is noted
that the observed frequencies of the beam with the exciter connected, correspond well with
the results of ViCoBeam.
When evaluating the observed response accelerations of the test beam at an excitation fre-
quency corresponding to the ’¼ürst vibration mode, it seems that the implementation of elastic
boundary conditions match well with observations. When comparing the measured accelera-
tions with Newmark time-histories, it seems that the implementation of both rigid cantilever
boundary conditions and elastic bi-moments result in poor correlation. The lower horizontal
accelerations of both boundary cases, corresponds to the previous ’¼ünding of the white-noise
test suggesting less sti’¼Ćbending boundary conditions along the horizontal z-axis. The imple-
mentation of elastic reaction bending moments seems to result in a time-history corresponding
well with observations, both at end- and mid-point. However, as previously noted, the di’¼Ćer-
ent boundary conditions in each bending direction is not fully permitable.
Observation of the response accelerations at other resonant frequencies does not match the
aforementioned ’¼ünding.
As shown in Figures 56 and 57 of Appendix E and summarized
33
==PAGE:48==
in Table 8, the observed response does not match the time-histories of any of the proposed
boundary conditions. This suggests that the resulting mode shape of the present theory given
these boundary conditions, are in disagreement with those of the actual beam. As previously
mentioned, the vertical- and horizontal acceleration both contain contributions from both the
torsional rotation and the respective translation. This hinders the study of the exact deviation
of the 3 degrees-of-freedom. Instead it can only concluded that there is some disagreement.
The exact nature of the disagreement is unknown. It is plausible that the aforementioned
boundary problems associated with varying sti’¼Ćness parameters KMz ╠Ė= KMy results in in-
correct mode shapes and therefore result in inaccurate Newmark time-histories. One possible
cause is that the connection of the beam and the vibration exciter a’¼Ćects the dynamic prop-
erties of the beam to an extend not adequately described by additional localized mass. If the
connection inhibits the vibration in any degree-of-freedom to any extend, it will inherently
in’¼éuence the vibration modes. The observed increase in natural frequency of the ’¼ürst two vi-
bration modes when connecting the exciter suggests that some unaccounted factor may indeed
be present. A factor that may in’¼éuence the calculated acceleration ratios, is the implemen-
tation of damping. Damping is implemented as Rayleigh damping as per Section 4.3.4. One
of the assumptions behind this, is that the 3 degrees-of-freedom are all subjects to the same
damping parameters ╬▒ and ╬▓. In practise it is possible that di’¼Ćerent ╬▒ and ╬▓ parameters are
applicable for each of the 3 DOFs. It is plausible that the sti’¼Ćness proportional parameter ╬▓
would a’¼Ćect the yield strain ╬│ of (4.35) at di’¼Ćerent level than the axial strain ╬Ą. An extended
study of the damping properties of the beam should be performed to con’¼ürm this. This would
require atleast 3 accelerometers to isolate the individual accelerations of the 3 DOFs. It is
possible that Rayleigh damping is a poor description of the actual damping properties of the
beam, which will inadvertently result in poor correlation of a time-integration when compared
to experimental results.
7.1
Future development
The future development of the present theory and implementation in ViCoBeam is self-
evident in the development of coupled bending mode shapes. The current implementation of
uncoupled mode shapes limits the utility of ViCoBeam to boundary conditions with identical
bending boundary conditions in each direction. The development of coupled mode shapes and
associated problems is brie’¼éy discussed in Appendix F. As the results of the Newmark time
integration does not correspond well with observations, work should be done to explain the
di’¼Ćerences. More detailed experimental veri’¼ücation is needed to identify the exact nature
of the deviations between the observations and the results of ViCoBeam. Speci’¼ücally more
accelerometers should be used to identify the speci’¼üc response of each DOF. In addition, future
experimental work should con’¼ürm that the practical implementation of boundary conditions
correspond well with the theoretical idealizations.
The expansion of the present theory to include rotary inertia is an additional candidate for
future development. Past studies by Arpaci et al [15] show signi’¼ücant inaccuracy in the deter-
mined natural frequencies when neglecting the rotary inertia for certain beams. The inclusion
of rotary inertia and the much less important warping inertia , is easily introduced by main-
taining the axial component of the kinetic energy when developing the energy formulation.
This addition will ultimately provide an additional term to the diagonal block matrices of the
mass matrix M. As shear deformation is neglected the bending rotation is described in terms
of the translational displacement, and the same un-coupled shape functions may be utilized.
It is noted that the rotary inertia depends on the ’¼ürst derivative of the translations ╬Šz, ╬Šy and
rotation Žå. The current un-coupled shape function does not imply orthogonality for these,
34
==PAGE:49==
and as a result the diagonal block matrices will not be diagonal. The mathematics behind
this is brie’¼éy covered in Appendix J.
By contrast, the expansion to include shear de’¼éection by Timoshenko beam theory, is a much
larger task. By making the shear de’¼éection a separate degree-of-freedom the size of the system
matrices will expand to 5N ├Ś 5N to incorporate the additional DOFs. In itself this is not
problematic, but the expansion will inherently require shape functions for the independent
shear vibration which is not immediately available, due to the inherent coupling with the
bending vibrations.
8
Conclusion
In the present study, the triply coupled bending-torsion vibration problem for a uniform
straight beam, has been solved by representing it as an eigenvalue problem. The present
theory is based on the Rayleigh-Ritz method, drawing inspiration from the work of Gere and
Lin [3]. The present solution includes warping e’¼Ćects but assumes that rotary inertia and
shear deformation e’¼Ćects are negligible. In addition it is assumed that the coupled vibration
modes may be accurately described as serial expansions of the shape functions of the un-
coupled vibration problem. This approximation is justi’¼üed by the same boundary conditions
applying to both the coupled and un-coupled vibration problem. The matrix formulation of
the vibration problem allows for time-history analysis by Newmark integration. This allows
for study of the forced vibration response. Speci’¼üc implementation of a point load and the
additional inertial e’¼Ćect that may follow practical installation, is described.
The theory is implemented within a Matlab program named ViCoBeam. The resulting
natural frequencies and mode shapes of a speci’¼üc beam featuring triple coupling, are compared
to numerical results of a ABAQUS ’¼ünite-element model. It is concluded that the results of
ViCoBeam is in agreement with the numerical results for a conventional cantilever beam.
The average discrepancy of the ’¼ürst 6 frequencies is approximately ŌłÆ2%, which is within the
expectations of the inherent simpli’¼ücation of Bernoulli-Euler beam theory. In addition it is
noted that signi’¼ücant deviations are found when describing a beam for which the bend problem
is de’¼üned from a di’¼Ćerent set of boundary conditions in each direction. This is attributed
to the adoption of none-principle axis in the description of the bending vibration problem,
which is shown to introduce alternate boundary conditions not ful’¼ülled by the un-coupled
shape functions.
Further veri’¼ücations of the present theory is performed experimentally. An asymmetric alu-
minium beam of identical cross-section to that of the ABAQUS model is designed. Welded
support conditions are utilized representing an idealized cantilever beam. A hammer stroke
test is performed to verify the natural frequencies of the beam by FFT analysis of the mea-
sured response accelerations. The observed frequencies are found to be considerably lower
than the results of the ViCoBeam program, suggesting overestimation of the beam sti’¼Ćness.
The same natural frequencies are studied by a white-noise test. The white-noise also serve
to highlight the change in natural frequencies associated with connecting a vibration exciter.
The resulting natural frequencies are found to decrease as expected as addition inertial e’¼Ćects
are added by the additional mass of the moving element and connecting joint. However, a
noticeable exception is the ’¼ürst two frequencies which are seen to increase. These increased
frequencies corresponds well with the results of ViCoBeam, but does not match the ex-
pectations of connecting the vibration exciter and cannot be immediately explained. The
theoretical boundary conditions of ViCoBeam are adjusted to ’¼üt the observed frequencies.
It is found that the implementation of elastic boundary conditions results in frequencies in
35
==PAGE:50==
agreement with observations. However, to accurately recover the experimental results, alter-
nate reaction bending moment sti’¼Ćness is implemented for the two directions which result in
di’¼Ćerent boundary conditions for each bending function. This is previously shown to cause
signi’¼ücant inaccuracies. Finally the speci’¼üc forced vibration response of harmonic loading is
studied. The beam is excited at a speci’¼üc resonant frequency and the resulting accelerations
recorded. This is compared with Newmark time-integration of similar forced response within
ViCoBeam. The measured magnitudes of the accelerations suggest a minimum damping
ratio of ╬Čmin = 0.024 which is quite signi’¼ücant. The ratio between vertical and horizontal
acceleration of the beam is compared with corresponding values of the Newmark time-history.
It is found that the ’¼ürst observed vibration mode corresponds well with the time-history of
the previously implemented elastic boundary conditions. However, the study of the forced
response at higher modes cannot reproduce this, and show poor correlation to the Newmark
time-history of any of the proposed boundary conditions.
Future work of the present theory should be focused on developing coupled shape functions
for the bending vibration problem, to allow distinct boundary conditions in each bending
direction, expanding the utility of the proposed theory. Additional work may be the inclusion
of rotary and warping inertia, which can be implemented with relative simplicity. The expan-
sion to include shear deformations require the addition of two degrees-of-freedom, which will
inherently provide complications as individual shape functions of the shear vibration problem
are required.
References
[1] Myklestad, N.O. 1945 American Society of Mechanical Engineers - Transactions 67, 61-
67. New Method of Calculating Natural Modes of Coupled Bending-Torsion Vibration of
Beams.
[2] Mendelson, A., Gendler, S. 1951 National Advisory Comitee for Aeronautics Report 1005.

A
Uncoupled shape functions
An essential part of the proposed solution of the coupled vibration problem, is the pre-de’¼üned
shape functions of the un-coupled bending and torsional vibration modes.
The following
appendix will present the method of derivation and examples of such shape functions. The
solution of the torsional problem follows the work of Gere [31].
To determine the shape functions, the homogeneous un-coupled equations of motions for both
bending and torsion are evaluated.

A.2.1
Example - Simple-Simple supported beam
The most simple set of boundary conditions are those of a simple-simple supported beam.
As for all sets of boundary conditions it is important to note that the boundary conditions
in terms of the rotation of the beam and separate and independent of those for the bending
problem.
In terms of rotation, a simple-simple beam refers to a beam with restricted rotation at both

A.2.2

of the torsional problem either. Warping deformations are especially di’¼ācult to fully restrain,
as the deformations are small (Appendix H).

reaction bi-moment will often give a better description of supported beam. A cantilever with
such elastic reaction bi-moment, have the following boundary conditions

B
Orthogonality conditions
Orthogonality is a convenient property of the vibration mode shapes, that allow for reduction
of expressions by uncoupling each individual vibration mode from the others. Two functions
f(x) and g(x) are considered orthogonal is their inner product Ō¤©f, gŌ¤®is equal to zero, if
f(x) ╠Ė= g(x). The inner product of two functions is de’¼üned as
Ō¤©f, gŌ¤®=
Z
f(x)g(x)dx
(B.1)
The following section will show that the shape functions of both the uncoupled bending and
the torsion problem, are indeed orthogonal. Given both bending directions are described by
the same uncoupled equations of motion, only one of the functions Z(x) will be described.
B.1

D
The Finite-Element model
The numerical validation of Section 5 has been performed using the commercial FEM program
- ABAQUS. The test-beam is modelled using shell elements and a eigenfrequency analysis
is run. Two di’¼Ćerent boundary conditions are tested - The cantilever set-up corresponding
to the experiment and a second con’¼üguration, with di’¼Ćerent bending boundary conditions in
each direction.
To verify the accuracy of the numerical model, a simple I-beam is modelled and compared
with analytical results. As an I-beam has no torsion-bending coupling, analytical frequencies
can be found using well known theory. Once it has been determined that the numerical model
can accurately produce the eigenfrequencies, the same modelling and element types is used
to model the test-beam.
D.1
Model veri’¼ücation
A simple I-beam is modelled in ABAQUS, and the resulting eigenfrequencies are compared
with analytical results. The I-beam cross-section can be seen in Figure 26. The corresponding
cross-section parameters can are seen in Table 11

The analytical eigenfrequencies are found by determining the wave numbers from the tran-

wave number and frequency can be found in (A.6) for both bending and torsional frequency
problem. For the investigated con’¼ügurations of boundary conditions, ’¼üxed-free, simple-simple
and ’¼üxed-’¼üxed boundary conditions are needed for the bending problem, while only ’¼üxed-free
and ’¼üxed-’¼üxed and needed for the torsion problem. The associated wave numbers found from
the transcendental equations of Tables 9 and 10 of Appendix A, can be seen in Table 12.
Note that unlike the bending wave numbers, the torsional wave numbers are dependant on
the sti’¼Ćness ratio k2 (A.6), and thus are speci’¼üc for this beam. The FEM model is constructed
from STRI65 shell elements. These elements enforce the Kirchho’¼Ćconstraint numerically and
are therefore only applicable for thin shells in which transverse shear deformation is negli-
gible. The STRI65 elements are 6 node triangular elements (3 corners and 3 midpoints),
each with 5 degrees of freedom (3 displacements and 2 in-plane rotations). The elements
are given appropriate thickness and material parameters (t = 3.0 ┬Ę 10ŌłÆ3 m, Žü = 2700 kg/ m3,
E = 70┬Ę109 Pa , ╬Į = 0.3). To model the ’¼üxed-free cantilever con’¼üguration, the one end of the
beam is restricted against all degrees-of-freedom, while the other remains free. This is one

The lack of di’¼Ćerence between the Matlab values and the analytical results is expected. An
I-beam, described in terms itŌĆÖs principle axis has no coupling of the di’¼Ćerent vibration modes,
making both the mass matrix M and sti’¼Ćness matrix K diagonal. As the mode shapes used
for the solutions are identical for both the analytical and the Matlab results - the results
becomes identical. It can be seen, that while good correlation is found between analytical
and the numerical results for the ’¼ürst vibration modes, signi’¼ücant di’¼Ćerence can be found for
higher order modes - especially for the strong-axis ’¼éexural vibration modes. This discrepancy
can be explained by simpli’¼ücations of the beam theory behind of the analytical results.
D.1.1
Limitations of Bernoulli-Euler theory
The analytical results are based on Bernoulli-Euler beam theory, which makes a number of
assumption for simplicity, namely that the beam cross section is perfectly rigid within itŌĆÖs own
plane, that plane sections remain plane, and that the beam cross-section is perpendicular to
the neutral axis. These constrains and the additional sti’¼Ćness they represent are not present
within the numerical model.
Figure 30: Warping e’¼Ćect due to transverse shear strains [32]
The last assumption of perpendicular cross-sections is addressed by Timoshenko beam theory
by introducing shear ’¼éexibility. The additional shear deformation, typically denoted ╬│ is pro-
portional to the change of the curvature along the beam d╬║
dx. As such, the e’¼Ćect of neglecting
the shear ’¼éexibility, is more signi’¼ücant for mode shapes with greater curvature changes, as
seen in the high order vibration modes. The increased sti’¼Ćness from neglecting this e’¼Ćect,
leads to higher natural frequencies and the inaccuracy increases at higher vibration modes.
That plane section remain plane, has been partially addressed by accounting for cross-section
warping due to torsion. However cross-section warping also occurs due to to bending shear
e’¼Ćects. A varying bending moment is accompanied by shear force as a necessity of equilibrium
of rotation. Similarly equilibrium of a solid requires vanishing shear stress at the free edges.
As a result the variation of angle is not constant across the cross-section and must therefore
result in warping [32]. This is illustrated in Figure 30.
Finally the assumption that the cross section is perfectly rigid within itŌĆÖs own plane is also
not present within the numerical model. cross-section distortion occur as a result of the shear
stress distribution over the cross section leading to local deformation as illustrated in Figure
31. This results in an additional reduction of sti’¼Ćness in comparison to the Bernoulli-Euler
solution, and as such a reduction in the natural frequencies. As this is also a shear deformation,
it is also proportional to the change of curvature ╬║ŌĆ▓, and therefore more prevalent in higher
vibration modes. The cross-section distortion can be considered a separate vibration mode
60
==PAGE:75==
(a) Shear strain distribution - Strong Axis
(b) Cross-section distortion - Strong Axis
(c) Shear strain distribution - Weak Axis
(d) Cross-section distortion - Weak Axis
Figure 31: Shear stress distribtion and Cross-section distortion of I-beam
with coupling to the ’¼éexural modes. Studying the higher vibration modes from the ABAQUS
analysis, the ’¼ürst of these distortional modes can be found as the 13th mode at a frequency
of f13 = 1287.6Hz. As explained by Barr & Duthie [22], only the ’¼ürst vibration mode of this
cross-section distortion is usually necessary to consider as the higher vibration modes occur
at much higher frequencies than the frequency ranged usually considered. While Timoshenko
Figure 32: Cross-section distortion vibration mode f13 = 1287.6Hz
beam theory elegantly accounts for shear ’¼éexibility, the e’¼Ćects of bending shear warping
and cross-section distortion are more complex and it is di’¼ācult to quantify which e’¼Ćects are
the predominant reason for the discrepancy between the numerical model and the analytical
61
==PAGE:76==
results of Table 13. Barr & Duthie [22] shows for an H-pro’¼üle, that the decrease in frequencies
in relation to Bernoulli-Euler beam theory, due to cross-sectional distortion, is greater than
the reduction due to Timoshenko shear ’¼éexibility at the higher vibration modes.
When evaluating the modes shapes of the ABAQUS model it is di’¼ācult to quantify which
e’¼Ćects are more prevalent.However, by evaluating the speci’¼üc nodal displacements of the
vibration mode shapes some evaluations can be made. For simplicity the following evaluations
are purely made for the strong axis ’¼éexural modes as this is where the discrepancy between
analytical and numerical frequencies is most prevalent.

The exact sti’¼Ćness reduction associated with these uncounted shear de’¼éections will not be
studied further, but the magnitude of the frequency discrepancy is important to consider
when evaluating the accuracy of the present theory of the coupled bending-torsion vibration
problem. It is clear that discrepancies at higher vibration modes will at least partially be
explained by the shortcomings of Bernoulli-Euler beam theory, while agreement should be
expected at the ’¼ürst vibration modes. The frequency discrepancy also suggests, that future
improvements to the proposed solution should consider shear deformations as it evidently
contributes to signi’¼ücant inaccuracies at the higher modes and therefore limits the utility of
the solution.

method, is to allow for di’¼Ćerent ’¼éexural boundary conditions in each direction. A cantilever
does not accomplish this, and an alternative boundary con’¼üguration must therefore be im-
plemented to verify the results in such a scenario. The proposed con’¼üguration of boundary

large discrepancies are found for the strong-axis ’¼éexural modes, even for the ’¼ürst mode.

mated shear strain (D.1) is plotted along the length of the beam, it is clear that the Timo-
shenko shear ’¼éexibility is signi’¼ücant. As previously explained, the added sti’¼Ćness associated
with neglecting this ’¼éexibility, leads to a increase in frequencies of the analytical results. The
e’¼Ćect is more prevalent for the strong axis ’¼éexural modes, as the higher bending sti’¼Ćness
EI, along with the ’¼üxed-’¼üxed boundary conditions, decreases the e’¼Ćective slenderness of the
beam making shear e’¼Ćects signi’¼ücant. As a result, the shear strain is much greater fot the
strong axis ’¼éexural modes than for the weak axis counter parts as seen in Figure 38. From
the graph it is evident, that despite frequencies in close proximity and that the weak axis
mode is of a higher order, the strong axis mode shows signi’¼ücantly higher shear strains.
Again this inherent frequency discrepancy from the Bernoulli-Euler assumptions, must be
considered when evaluating the results of the proposed solution to the coupled vibration
problem.

is meant as an additional comparison to the experimental results, while the simply supported
model is meant to verify the proposed solution in alternative boundary conditions. The ’¼ünal
set-up is intended to study the application of the proposed solution in cases with di’¼Ćerent

D.2.2
Simple supported beam
In addition to the two beams modelled in Section 5, a simple supported beam con’¼üguration
is modelled using this same method as for the I-beam, by modelling a rigid end plate on
the beam. Through implemented boundary conditions, the end plates are restrained from
displacements and axial rotation at the centroid on the beam. This allows ’¼éexural rotation in
both directions while the torsional rotation is restrained. This ensures the beam to have the
’¼éexural behaviour of traditional simply supported beam, while having the torsional behaviour
of a ’¼üxed-’¼üxed beam, due to the rigid end plates restricting warping.

D.2.3
Simple-’¼üxed combination
The ’¼ünal boundary condition con’¼üguration that is studied, is the combination of a simple-
simple and a ’¼üxed-’¼üxed supported beam. The goal of this is to see if the present theory
and ViCoBeam allows for di’¼Ćerent shape functions for the two bending directions.
As
explained in detail within Appendix F, describing the beam in terms of none-principal (z, y)-
axes introduce some boundary problems. The comparison with a numerical model is intended
to study the impact of these issues.
The boundary conditions are modelled using the same rigid end plates as earlier. As for the I-
beam, the boundary conditions are implemented along a line through the centroid, restricting
displacements and the appropriate rotations. The 4 ’¼ürst modes can be seen in Figure 42. In
extension of Table 3 in Section D.1, the resulting ’¼ürst 9 natural frequencies are seen in Table

E
Experimental set-up
The test set-up of the experiment can be seen in Figure 43.
The set-up consists of an
asymmetric aluminium beam, designed to match the cross-section of Figure 10 in Section D.1,
with a length of 1.00 m. To ’¼ünd accurate experimental results corresponding to those of the
present theory in ViCoBeam, the actual supports of the beams should match the theoretical
boundary conditions. Experimental veri’¼ücation in other studies (Ambrosini [25]) have ensured
vanishing bending rotation of ’¼üxed boundary conditions, by applying large vertical loads.
While e’¼Ćective at ’¼üxing the transverse displacement, itŌĆÖs e’¼Ćectiveness at restraining the small
axial deformation associated with torsional warping is not well described.
Figure 43: Illustration of experiment set-up
For this study, a welded connection to a 10 mm think end plate is chosen as seen in Figure
44. This is done to restrict the axial deformation of the warping. Static calculations of the
stresses within the welds are done in Appendix I for conservative displacements and rotations,
to ensure that the welds have adequate strength to resist the forces generated by a resonant
response.
(a) Weld below beam
(b) Weld at top of beam
Figure 44: The welded end-plate connection
The end plate is fastened to a an aluminium supported frame by bolts as seen in Figure 44.
The support frame is bolted to the test table so fully restrain any movement of the set-up.
The technical drawings of the beam along with the support frame can be found in Appendix
I. To measure the vibrations, two MEMS DC accelerometers are ’¼ütted to the beam, recording
71
==PAGE:86==
the vertical and horizontal acceleration mounted using adhesive tape. The accelerometers are
model 3741B1210G from PCB Piezotronics with gas-damped, silicon MEMS sensing elements.
The accelerometers have a measurement range of ┬▒10 g = ┬▒98.1 m
s2 at a frequency range of
0-1000 Hz. The resonant frequencies of the accelerometers themselves are above frequencies
of 2000 Hz, and should not be encountered within the testing range. The sensitivity at low
frequencies is su’¼ācient to allow for measurements of the lowest vibration modes. Calibration
data from a back-to-back comparison, show that deviations of measured accelerations, occurs
at frequencies above 250 Hz with 0.3% deviation at 300 Hz, upto 2,6 Hz at 1000 Hz. This
should be accounted for through calibration of measurements.
(a) Exact end-point location
(b) Attached accelerometers at mid-point
Figure 45: Location and attachment of accelerometers
With a mass of 10g each, the additional mass of the accelerometers have minimal e’¼Ćect on
the natural frequencies. However, to maximize accuracy, the additional mass is accounted for
through the the procedure described in Section 4.3.3. The accelerometers are powered through
separate DC power-supplies. The accelerometers features supply voltage regulation, allowing
for excitation voltages between 6 to 30 VDC, Thus providing versatility in the choice of power-
supply. However, both accelerometers cannot be powered by a single power-supply, as this was
found to causes both accelerometers to measure the mean acceleration of both accelerometers,
thus making it impossible to distinguish the accelerations in each direction. Data acquisition
is done as the accelerometers feed a DC output of 20.46
mV
m/s2 and 20.37
mV
m/s2 respectively,
through a DS1104 dSpace R&D controller board connected to a desktop computer.
The
signals are sampled at a rate of 1000 data points per second. This allows for more than
6 data points per oscillation up to a frequency of 165 Hz to minimize aliasing. At higher
frequencies aliasing may occur and should be considered when evaluated the measurement of
the higher vibration modes. The data is recorded over varying time intervals as needed, and
saved as a .mat ’¼üles to be processed within Matlab.
The experiment is done in three parts. A hammer stroke test, a white noise test, and excitation
of speci’¼üc frequencies. For the hammer stroke test, the accelerometers are mounted at the
free end of the beam. For the remaining tests, the accelerometers are place in two di’¼Ćerent
locations; at the free end and at the beam mid point. For the last two parts of the experiment,
a Type 4809 Vibration Exciter from Br┬©uel & Kj├”r is used. The exciter can a apply a harmonic
force of upto 44.5 N without assisted cooling at an input current of 5 A RMS, in a frequency
range of 10 Hz - 20 kHz. With a head constant of 6.4N
A , the exciter allows for precise control
of the peak force by controlling the input current.
The exciter has a dynamic weight of
its moving elements of 60g which should be accounted for, as this additional localized mass
vibrates along with the beam. However, as it only moves along one axis, it should only be
72
==PAGE:87==
included for the vibration of the vertical degree-of-freedom.
(a) Vibration exciter
(b) Power Ampli’¼üer
Figure 46: Vibration exciter and power ampli’¼üer
The Exciter has a maximum allowable peak-to-peak displacement of 8 mm. Emergency end
stops are implemented internally in the unit, but should not be relied on. This puts limitations
on the allowable acceleration at frequencies lower than 70 Hz. At frequencies above 70 Hz,
the accelerations will be limited by the force available, as the mass of the moving element
alone will reduce the maximum displacements to below the limits. In case of resonance, the
beam vibrations may pull on the moving element. To ensure this does not cause damage to
the unit, a threaded insert in used to act as a mechanical fuse. The insert will strip itŌĆÖs inner
thread before excessive force can damage the unit.
Figure 47: Illustration of experiment set-up
To control the input current, a Br┬©uel & Kj├”r Type 2718 Power Ampli’¼üer is used.
The
Power Ampli’¼üer allows for control of the ampli’¼ücation of the sinusoidal signal generated by
the dSpace controller. An upper current limit is speci’¼üed, limiting the input current to the
vibration exciter to the allowable limit of 5A RMS. The power ampli’¼üer also features a LCD
display, allowing the exact input current and voltage to be read. This is essential to determine
the magnitude of the applied force. An illustration of the experiment set-up can be seen in
Figure 47.
To transfer the harmonic load from the exciter to the beam, a model 616.303 double Cardan
joint from Brd Klee is utilized as seen in Figure 48.
This allows for vertical transfer of
73
==PAGE:88==
the load while permitting horizontal translation and axial rotation. Due to the kinematic
connecting to the bend, the joint will vibrate along with the beam. This provides additional
mass to the system which should be added as per Section 4.3.3. While the entire mass of
the joint contributes to the vertical vibration, only part of it will contribute to the horizontal
and rotational vibrations. This is implemented by adjusting the contribution to each DOF,
through the mf matrix.
At a weight of 90g for the joint, and a weight of 60g for the
moving element of the vibration exciter, the vertical DOF is prescribed the full 150g while
the horizontal is prescribed an estimated 50g. At the location at the bottom ’¼éange the joint
has an eccentricity of (emz = 25.8 mm, emy = ŌłÆ12.1 mm).
(a) Double Cardan joint [33]
(b) Installed Joint
Figure 48: Load Transferring joint
E.1
Hammer stroke experiment
The Hammer stroke experiment is performed by striking the beam a hammer to excite the
natural frequencies of the beam. The test is performed before connecting the vibration exciter
to eliminate any changes to the dynamic system caused by this.
As each strike excites
vibrations only for a brief period of time, limited data can be acquired for each strike. To
eliminate potential errors, the test is repeated multiple times (18), with blows at various
locations of the beam as shown in Figure 49 using two di’¼Ćerent hammers - a plastic- and a
steel hammer.
(a) Location along length of beam [mm]
(b) Location on cross-section
Figure 49: Location of hammer blows on beam
The various locations of hammer strikes, ensures that all modes are excited. Certain modes
may not be excited with a single strike, as they may have zero-crossings of one or more of
the DOFs at the location of the hammer strike. Analogously, the beam is struck on di’¼Ćerent
74
==PAGE:89==
locations on the cross section to make sure each DOF is represented. The two hammers used
to the test are seen in Figure 50.
Figure 50: The steel and plastic hammer used for the test
The natural frequencies of the beam are measured by performing a Fast Fourier Transform
on the measured accelerations. The resulting power density spectrum is analysed for within
the 0-500 Hz range. The PSDs of the 18 tests contain several peaks representing each natural
frequency. The logarithmic PSDs of Figure 14 are chosen as they are a good representation
of the spectres.
E.2
White-noise test
To further study the dynamic response of the beam, the exciter is connected, to allow the
response at speci’¼üc frequencies to be studied. However, by connecting the vibration exciter,
some properties of the system will inherently change. Speci’¼ücally the e’¼Ćective mass of the
system will change as the mass of both the connecting Cardan joint and the moving element
of the exciter will provide additional inertial forces as discussed previously.
Therefore a direct comparison to the frequencies of the hammer test is not possible. Instead, a
white-noise test is performed, to determine the equivalent natural frequencies of the expanded
system. The white-noise test is performed by generating a white noise signal and sending this
Figure 51: Simulink model - White noise test
through the vibration exciter.
White-noise is a random noise signal with constant power
spectral density. As such, all frequencies are excited. The beam will naturally have greater
response at the natural frequencies, making it possible to determine these from the response
PSD. The white noise is generated using the Matlab Simulink ŌĆØBand-limited White NoiseŌĆØ
source block. The The sample-rate of the white noise generator is set to match the data
75
==PAGE:90==
recording at 0.001s. The Simulink model (Figure 51) is transferred to the dSpace control
board which communicates with both the vibration exciter and accelerometers.
The response PSD of the beam is found using the Fast-Fourier Transform as previously. One
of the advantages of a white-noise test over a hammer test, is that the excitation and data
recording can be done over an extended period of time. Whereas the hammer stroke test is
limited to data within the few seconds it takes for the beam to return to itŌĆÖs static state,
the white-noise test can be run for as long as the data acquisition allows. This means that
fewer tests are needed to produce accurate results. While 18 tests were made for the hammer
stroke test, the white-noise test is performed only 6 times over a period of 60 seconds - three
with accelerometers at xaccel = 0.98m and three with xaccel = 0.50m. The mean frequencies
can be seen in Table 5. A sample logarithmic PSD of the vertical and horizontal white-noise
response of the beam can be seen in Figure 15 of Section E.2, both for xaccel = 0.98m.
E.3
Excitation of speci’¼üc frequencies
To study the speci’¼üc vibration modes, the response of the beam given speci’¼üc resonant
excitation frequencies are studied. The test is performed by generating a sinusoidal signal
within the Simulink model, and exporting it to the dSpace controller. The Simulink model
can be seen in Figure 52. The frequency and amplitude of the sine-wave can be adjusted
through the dSpace control interface, allowing for precise frequencies to be excited.
Figure 52: Simulink model - Excitation test
The amplitude of the signal is controlled so that it matches a speci’¼üc peak force. Given
the acceleration limits of the accelerometers of ┬▒ 10g, the peak force must be adjusted for
each frequency. The peak force is used for generating the loading vector used within the
Newmark time-integration. The resulting magnitudes of the Newmark integration is compared
with the experimental results and the damping parameters are adjusted accordingly.
In
addition to the study of the response accelerations, the frequency ’¼ündings of the white-noise
test are also veri’¼üed, by measuring the maximum acceleration of the response over a range
frequencies within the expected range.
Unlike the FFT analysis, this direct study of the
response eliminates any arti’¼ücial apparent resonant frequencies due to aliasing of high order
vibration modes.
Sample measurement of such frequency analysis can be seen in Figure
53 where the accelerations are measured for a gradually decreasing excitation frequency at
intervals of ŌłåŽēf = 1rad/s.
It is clear that the accelerations increase considerably around the natural frequency as ex-
pected. In addition it can be seen that sudden spikes in accelerations occur when changing
