Once the precise frequency of the vibration mode is determined, the magnitude of the accel-
erations are studied. The measured accelerations at an excitation frequency of Žē1 = 154 are
plotted in Figure 16a of Section 6.3. When processing the measurements of the accelerom-
eters, itŌĆÖs important to ensure that the sign convention of the measurements correspond to
those implemented within ViCoBeam. At in input current of 0.9 A, a peak force of 5.5 N
is applied. This is implemented in ViCoBeam as a point load as described in Section 4.3.2.
With a load eccentricity in the z-direction of e1 = 25.8 mm, this implies a peak applied torque
of 141.9 Nmm. This is implemented in the calculation of the f(t) load vector for the Newmark
integration. As the dynamic accelerations given a harmonic load increase as function of the
square of the frequency, the peak force of 5.5 N causes accelerations beyond the maximum
allowable limit for higher frequencies. As such the input current is reduced to 0.3 A, equiva-
lent of a peak force of 1.9 N. The measured response accelerations of the ’¼ürst vibration mode
are found in Figure 16 and 17 of Section 6.3 for the end- and mid-point accelerations, along
with the time-histories of corresponding ViCoBeam solutions. The response accelerations
at excitations of Žēf = 519 rad/s and Žēf = 750 rad/s can be found in Figure 56 and 57 along
with ViCoBeam results.
While the observations of the ’¼ürst vibration mode corresponds
reasonably with the ViCoBeam results of a cantilever beam with elastic reaction moments,
it is clear that the same correlation cannot be seen for the accelerations of Figure 56 and 57.
Noticeably it can be seen that the end-point horizontal accelerations at Žēf = 519 rad/s are
much lower than any ViCoBeam results. By contrast the mid-point horizontal accelerations
at Žēf = 750 rad/s are much larger than any of the theoretical time-histories.

F
The use and limitations of uncoupled shape functions
The primary assumption for the proposed solution method, is that linear combination of the
shape functions of the uncoupled vibration problem, can be utilized as approximations of the
coupled mode shapes, as the same boundary conditions are ful’¼ülled. This is an assumption
made by Gere & Lin [3] and is accurate while describing beam along principle axis. However,
it may be advantageous to describe the beam in terms of a di’¼Ćerent coordinate system, either
due to loading or due to boundary conditions. This introduces the coupling sti’¼Ćness EIzy,
and couples the two bending functions through sti’¼Ćness relations thus introducing boundary
conditions, not present within the uncoupled vibration problem.
If both sets of ’¼éexural
boundary conditions are identical, the assumption is a accurate.
However, if the ’¼éexural
boundary conditions vary in each direction, inaccuracies will occur as shown in Section D.1.
The following section explores the problems that occur by utilizing the uncoupled shape
functions.
F.1
Coupled vibration problem by equations of motion
The boundary condition problem of using the uncoupled shape functions becomes evident
when deriving the eigenvalue problem from the equations of motion. The system of governing
equations of motion for the coupled vibration problem, is derived in terms of the principle
axis by Gere & Lin [3] through use of dŌĆÖAlembertŌĆÖs principle of substituting the inertia forces
into the equations of static equilibrium.

F.2
Coupled shape functions
While actual coupled shape functions will not be derive in this section, the coupled spacial
problem and the additional boundary conditions which must be satis’¼üed are studied to explore
possible improvements to the proposed solution method.
F.2.1
The expanded boundary conditions
To explore the additional boundary conditions associated with the coupled bending prob-
lem, the static equilibrium equations are studied as these shows the origin of the boundary
conditions. To derive the static equilibrium equations, the potential energy balance of the
static problem is considered as per Krenk [26]. The potential energy consist of two parts; the
strain energy and the external work of the loading. The strain energy follows the derivation
of Section 2.3. The strain energy for unit length of the beam, has the following expression,
when exclusively considering the strain energy associated with bending, and eliminating the
contributions from the static moments by appropriate choice of bending rotation center.

F.2.2
The coupled equation of motion
The coupled equations of motion for the bending problem can be found using dŌĆÖAlembertŌĆÖs
principle of adding the inertia forces to the governing di’¼Ćerential equation of the static equi-
librium (F.2.1). This gives the following homogeneous equations of motion, by neglecting the
external loading.

(F.27)
Ordinarily the shape functions would be determined by using separation of variables, thus
separating the vibration problem into spacial and temporal parts. However, using this ap-
proach for the coupled problem is not without issues. The transverse displacement functions
╬Šz(x, t) and ╬Šy(x, t) are assumed to be the product of a spacial and a temporal function.
╬Šz(x, t) = Z(x)rz(t)
╬Šy(x, t) = Y (x)ry(t)
(F.28)
Inserting (F.2.2) into (F.2.2) the following set of equations are found
EIzZŌĆ▓ŌĆ▓ŌĆ▓ŌĆ▓(x)rz(t) + EIzyY ŌĆ▓ŌĆ▓ŌĆ▓ŌĆ▓(x)ry(t) + ŽüA Z(x)┬©rz(t) = 0
EIyY ŌĆ▓ŌĆ▓ŌĆ▓ŌĆ▓(x)ry(t) + EIzyZŌĆ▓ŌĆ▓ŌĆ▓ŌĆ▓(x)rz(t) + ŽüA Y (x)┬©ry(t) = 0
(F.29)
The temporal functions are collected on the right side, while the spacial are collected on the
left side.

While the sti’¼Ćness ratios ╬╗z and ╬╗y are well de’¼üned, the Žä(t) ratio between the temporal
solutions is unknown. As the temporal solutions are unknown the ratio between them are
equally unknown. While it seem reasonable that the two temporal solutions are in phase for
a given vibration mode, it is not known whether the ratio remains constant. As such, Žä(t)
remains a function of time, thus hindering the intention of separate variables. As such an
alternate solution method must be utilized solve the coupled vibration problem.

Newmark integration
The matrix formulation of the vibration problem, allows for time-integration by the Newmark
procedure.

derivative ╦Öx(t) over the time interval.

Warping deformation
Warping deformation is known for being associated with very small de’¼éections. As such it
is inherently di’¼ācult to secure perfect or near perfect rigidity for practical implementations
of retrained warping boundary conditions. To illustrate this, the axial warping deformation
associated with a conservative torsional rotation of a cantilever beam is calculated. According
to Krenk [26], the axial deformation at a speci’¼üc point s on the cross-section may be calculated
as
w(s) = ŌłÆŽē(s)dŽå(z)

(H.1)

When evaluating the actual weld of the beam
it can be seen that a marginal gap exists between the end-plate and the beam as seen in
Figure 62. The gap is not quite big enough to ’¼üt a standard piece of paper at a thickness of
approximately 0.1 mm. Negative warping deformations - that is retraction from the end plate
- will naturally not be restricted by the presence of the end-plate. However, the gap suggest
that positive warping deformation will similarly beuna’¼Ćected by the end-plate given the size
of the axial de’¼éections. Instead, warping deformations are purely restricted by the welds
themselves. While the warping deformations are clearly not un-restricted, it is plausible, that
Figure 62: Gap at weld
due to the minimal size of the deformations, the welds will not fully restrict warping to a
degree that can be considered rigid. Therefore, an elastic bi-moment boundary condition is
a more appropriate boundary condition than a rigid cantilever.

J
Rotary inertia
The present section aim to explore the expansion of the present theory to include the addi-
tional inertial e’¼Ćects associated with bending rotation. Past studies by Arpaci et al [15] have
highlighted the importance of including rotary inertia. The expansion of the present theory
to include the rotary - and the much less prevalent warping - inertia e’¼Ćects is achieved by
conserving the axial component of the displacement in the expression of the kinetic energy of
the beam.
T = 1
2Žü
Z
L
Z
A
╦Öu2 + ╦Öv2 + ╦Öw2dAdL
(J.1)
The derivation of the translation components u and v remain identical to that of Section 2.2.
As such, only the derivation of the axial kinetic energy is covered in the present section. The
axial kinetic energy per unit length may be expressed as
1

(J.4)
If the serial expansion of the displacements (3.1) is utilized, the total axial kinetic energy Tw
may be written as the following matrix product.
Tw = 1

(J.7)
The total kinetic energy of the system may then be expressed as

(J.8)
99
==PAGE:114==
where M refers to the original mass matrix of Section 3, while M+ is the mass matrix of the
expanded system. The eigenvalue problem may be solved identically to previously using the
new mass matrix.
It is noted that while orthogonality conditions of the un-coupled shape functions were utilized
to reduce the original mass matrix M considerably, the same cannot be done for the rotary
mass matrix Mr. This is because the current shape functions are derived from the equation
of motions for the vibration problem without rotary and warping inertia. As the inclusion
of the additional inertial e’¼Ćects have no in’¼éuence on the boundary conditions, it is assumed
that the current shape function for the simpli’¼üed problem are accurate approximations of the
actual shape functions.
Table 20 show the e’¼Ćect of including the additional inertial e’¼Ćect for the test beam for N = 10.
It clear that the e’¼Ćect is minimal for this beam, especially at the lowest vibration modes.
